Beneficial for this purpose



 | 

 4 !
 



Little to no effect



 | 
 Overwintering roost.
 [[132]](#cite_note-Robert_Michael_Pyle-132)
 Information on native range, cultivation requirements, bloom time and any other criteria that might lead a gardener to select this species over the others.
 

 The
 **pecan** 
[Template:IPAc-en](/w/index.php?title=Template:IPAc-en&action=edit&redlink=1 "Template:IPAc-en (does not exist)") 
 (
 *Carya illinoinensis* 
 ) is a species of
 [hickory](/w/index.php?title=Hickory&action=edit&redlink=1 "Hickory (does not exist)") 
 native to northern Mexico and the southern United States in the region of the
 [Mississippi River](/w/index.php?title=Mississippi_River&action=edit&redlink=1 "Mississippi River (does not exist)") 
 .
 [[52]](#cite_note-grin-52)
 The tree is cultivated for its seed in the southern United States, primarily in
 [Georgia](/w/index.php?title=Georgia_(U.S._state)&action=edit&redlink=1 "Georgia (U.S. state) (does not exist)") 
[[133]](#cite_note-ug-133)
 and Texas,
 [[134]](#cite_note-134)


 The pecan tree is a large
 [deciduous](/w/index.php?title=Deciduous&action=edit&redlink=1 "Deciduous (does not exist)") 
[tree](/w/index.php?title=Tree&action=edit&redlink=1 "Tree (does not exist)") 
 , growing to 20–40 m (66–130 ft) in height, rarely to 44 m (144 ft).
 [[73]](#cite_note-fna-73)
 It typically has a spread of 12–23 m (39–75 ft) with a trunk up to 2 m (6.6 ft) diameter. A 10-year-old sapling grown in optimal conditions will stand about 5 m (16 ft) tall. The
 [leaves](/w/index.php?title=Leaf&action=edit&redlink=1 "Leaf (does not exist)") 
 are alternate, 30–45 cm (12–18 in) long, and
 [pinnate](/w/index.php?title=Pinnate&action=edit&redlink=1 "Pinnate (does not exist)") 
 with 9–17 leaflets, each leaflet 5–12 cm (2.0–4.7 in) long and 2–6 cm (0.79–2.4 in) broad.
 [[73]](#cite_note-fna-73)


 The seeds of the pecan are edible, with a rich, buttery flavor. They can be eaten fresh or used in cooking, particularly in sweet desserts, such as
 [pecan pie](/w/index.php?title=Pecan_pie&action=edit&redlink=1 "Pecan pie (does not exist)") 
 , a traditional
 [Southern U.S. dish](/w/index.php?title=Cuisine_of_the_Southern_United_States&action=edit&redlink=1 "Cuisine of the Southern United States (does not exist)") 
 . Pecans are the major ingredient in
 [praline](/w/index.php?title=Praline&action=edit&redlink=1 "Praline (does not exist)") 
[candy](/w/index.php?title=Candy&action=edit&redlink=1 "Candy (does not exist)") 
 .
 [[135]](#cite_note-135)

 Information on native range, cultivation requirements, bloom time and any other criteria that might lead a gardener to select this species over the others.
 

***Maclura pomifera***
 , commonly known as the
 **Osage orange** 
 , hedge, or hedge apple tree is a small
 [deciduous](/w/index.php?title=Deciduous&action=edit&redlink=1 "Deciduous (does not exist)") 
[tree](/w/index.php?title=Tree&action=edit&redlink=1 "Tree (does not exist)") 
 or large
 [shrub](/w/index.php?title=Shrub&action=edit&redlink=1 "Shrub (does not exist)") 
 , typically growing to 8 to 15 meters (30–50 ft) tall. The distinctive fruit, from a
 [multiple fruit](/w/index.php?title=Multiple_fruit&action=edit&redlink=1 "Multiple fruit (does not exist)") 
 family, is roughly spherical, bumpy, 3 to 6 inches (8–15 cm) in diameter, and turns bright yellow-green in the fall. The fruits secrete a sticky white
 [latex](/wiki/Latex "Latex") 
 when cut or damaged. Despite the name "Osage orange",
 [[136]](#cite_note-wynia-136)


 Mature trees range from 40 to 65 feet (12–20 m) tall with short trunks and round-topped canopies.
 [[136]](#cite_note-wynia-136)
 The roots are thick, fleshy, and covered with bright orange bark. The tree's mature bark is dark, deeply furrowed and scaly. The plant has significant potential to invade unmanaged habitats.
 [[136]](#cite_note-wynia-136)



 As a
 [dioecious](/w/index.php?title=Dioecy&action=edit&redlink=1 "Dioecy (does not exist)") 
 plant, the inconspicuous
 [pistillate](/w/index.php?title=Pistillate&action=edit&redlink=1 "Pistillate (does not exist)") 
 (female) and
 [staminate](/w/index.php?title=Staminate&action=edit&redlink=1 "Staminate (does not exist)") 
 (male) flowers are found on different trees.
 

 The fruit has a
 [cucumber](/w/index.php?title=Cucumber&action=edit&redlink=1 "Cucumber (does not exist)") 
 -like flavor.
 [[137]](#cite_note-Ghosts_Evo-137)


 Osage orange's pre-Columbian range was largely restricted to a small area in what is now the United States, namely the
 [Red River](/w/index.php?title=Red_River_of_the_South&action=edit&redlink=1 "Red River of the South (does not exist)") 
 drainage of
 [Oklahoma](/w/index.php?title=Oklahoma&action=edit&redlink=1 "Oklahoma (does not exist)") 
 ,
 [Texas](/w/index.php?title=Texas&action=edit&redlink=1 "Texas (does not exist)") 
 , and
 [Arkansas](/w/index.php?title=Arkansas&action=edit&redlink=1 "Arkansas (does not exist)") 
 , as well as the
 [Blackland Prairies](/w/index.php?title=Texas_blackland_prairies&action=edit&redlink=1 "Texas blackland prairies (does not exist)") 
 and
 [post oak savannas](/w/index.php?title=Oak_savanna&action=edit&redlink=1 "Oak savanna (does not exist)") 
 .
 [[136]](#cite_note-wynia-136)
 A disjunct population also occurred in the
 [Chisos Mountains](/w/index.php?title=Chisos_Mountains&action=edit&redlink=1 "Chisos Mountains (does not exist)") 
 of Texas.
 [[138]](#cite_note-USDA-138)
 It has since become widely naturalized in the United States and Ontario, Canada.
 [[136]](#cite_note-wynia-136)
 Osage orange has been planted in all the 48 contiguous states of the United States and in southeastern Canada.
 [[138]](#cite_note-USDA-138)


 The fruit is not poisonous to humans or livestock, but is not preferred by them
 [[139]](#cite_note-IPM_IowaU_2-139)
 because it is mostly inedible due to a large size (about the diameter of a
 [softball](/w/index.php?title=Softball&action=edit&redlink=1 "Softball (does not exist)") 
 ), and hard, dry texture.
 [[137]](#cite_note-Ghosts_Evo-137)
 The edible seeds of the fruit are used by
 [squirrels](/w/index.php?title=Squirrel&action=edit&redlink=1 "Squirrel (does not exist)") 
 as food. Large animals such as
 [livestock](/w/index.php?title=Livestock&action=edit&redlink=1 "Livestock (does not exist)") 
 , which typically would consume fruits and disperse seeds, mainly ignore the fruit.
 [[137]](#cite_note-Ghosts_Evo-137)


 The fruits are consumed by black-tailed deer in Texas and fox squirrels in the Midwest, who drop them to crack open. Crossbills are said to peck the seeds out.
 [[140]](#cite_note-Peattie-140)


*Maclura pomifera* 
 prefers a deep and fertile soil, but is hardy over most of the contiguous United States, where it is used as a
 [hedge](/w/index.php?title=Hedge&action=edit&redlink=1 "Hedge (does not exist)") 
 . It must be regularly pruned to keep it in bounds, and the shoots of a single year will grow 3 to 6 feet (1–2 m) long, making it suitable for
 [coppicing](/w/index.php?title=Coppicing&action=edit&redlink=1 "Coppicing (does not exist)") 
 .
 [[141]](#cite_note-Keeler-141)
[[142]](#cite_note-Toensmeier-142)
 A neglected hedge will become fruit-bearing. It is remarkably free from insect predators and fungal diseases.
 [[141]](#cite_note-Keeler-141)
 A thornless male cultivar of the species exists and is vegetatively reproduced for ornamental use.
 [[138]](#cite_note-USDA-138)
*M. pomifera* 
 is cultivated in Italy, former Yugoslavia, Romania, former USSR, and India.
 [[143]](#cite_note-Elsevier_dict-143)

***Morus rubra***
 , commonly known as the
 **red mulberry** 
 , is a species of
 [mulberry](/w/index.php?title=Morus_(plant)&action=edit&redlink=1 "Morus (plant) (does not exist)") 
 native to
 [eastern](/w/index.php?title=Eastern_United_States&action=edit&redlink=1 "Eastern United States (does not exist)") 
 and central
 [North America](/w/index.php?title=North_America&action=edit&redlink=1 "North America (does not exist)") 
 . It is found from
 [Ontario](/w/index.php?title=Ontario&action=edit&redlink=1 "Ontario (does not exist)") 
 ,
 [Minnesota](/w/index.php?title=Minnesota&action=edit&redlink=1 "Minnesota (does not exist)") 
 , and
 [Vermont](/w/index.php?title=Vermont&action=edit&redlink=1 "Vermont (does not exist)") 
 south to southern
 [Florida](/w/index.php?title=Florida&action=edit&redlink=1 "Florida (does not exist)") 
 , and west as far as southeastern
 [South Dakota](/w/index.php?title=South_Dakota&action=edit&redlink=1 "South Dakota (does not exist)") 
 ,
 [Nebraska](/w/index.php?title=Nebraska&action=edit&redlink=1 "Nebraska (does not exist)") 
 ,
 [Kansas](/w/index.php?title=Kansas&action=edit&redlink=1 "Kansas (does not exist)") 
 , and central
 [Texas](/w/index.php?title=Texas&action=edit&redlink=1 "Texas (does not exist)") 
 . There have been reports of isolated populations (very likely naturalized) in
 [New Mexico](/w/index.php?title=New_Mexico&action=edit&redlink=1 "New Mexico (does not exist)") 
 ,
 [Idaho](/w/index.php?title=Idaho&action=edit&redlink=1 "Idaho (does not exist)") 
 , and
 [British Columbia](/w/index.php?title=British_Columbia&action=edit&redlink=1 "British Columbia (does not exist)") 
 .
 [[144]](#cite_note-144)



 Common in the
 [United States](/w/index.php?title=United_States&action=edit&redlink=1 "United States (does not exist)") 
 , it is listed as an
 [endangered species](/w/index.php?title=Endangered_species&action=edit&redlink=1 "Endangered species (does not exist)") 
 in
 [Canada](/w/index.php?title=Canada&action=edit&redlink=1 "Canada (does not exist)") 
 ,
 [[73]](#cite_note-fna-73)
[[145]](#cite_note-ambrose-145)
 and is susceptible to
 [hybridization](/w/index.php?title=Hybrid_(biology)&action=edit&redlink=1 "Hybrid (biology) (does not exist)") 
 with the
 [invasive](/w/index.php?title=Invasive_species&action=edit&redlink=1 "Invasive species (does not exist)") 
[white mulberry](/w/index.php?title=White_mulberry&action=edit&redlink=1 "White mulberry (does not exist)") 
 (
 *M. alba* 
 ), introduced from
 [Asia](/w/index.php?title=Asia&action=edit&redlink=1 "Asia (does not exist)") 
 .
 [[146]](#cite_note-burgess-146)


 Red mulberry is a
 [deciduous](/w/index.php?title=Deciduous&action=edit&redlink=1 "Deciduous (does not exist)") 
[tree](/w/index.php?title=Tree&action=edit&redlink=1 "Tree (does not exist)") 
 , growing to
 [Template:Cvt](/w/index.php?title=Template:Cvt&action=edit&redlink=1 "Template:Cvt (does not exist)") 
 tall, rarely
 [Template:Cvt](/w/index.php?title=Template:Cvt&action=edit&redlink=1 "Template:Cvt (does not exist)") 
 , with a trunk up to
 [Template:Cvt](/w/index.php?title=Template:Cvt&action=edit&redlink=1 "Template:Cvt (does not exist)") 
 in diameter. It is a small to medium-sized
 [[147]](#cite_note-147)
 tree that reaches a height of 70 feet and lives up to 125 years.
 

 The fruit is a compound cluster of several small
 [achenes](/w/index.php?title=Achene&action=edit&redlink=1 "Achene (does not exist)") 
 surrounded by a fleshy calyx, similar in appearance to a
 [blackberry](/w/index.php?title=Blackberry&action=edit&redlink=1 "Blackberry (does not exist)") 
 ,
 [Template:Cvt](/w/index.php?title=Template:Cvt&action=edit&redlink=1 "Template:Cvt (does not exist)") 
 long. It is initially pale green, ripening to red or dark purple.
 [[73]](#cite_note-fna-73)
 The berries are widely sought after by birds in spring and early summer in North America; as many as 31 species of birds have been recorded visiting a fruiting tree in Arkansas.
 [[148]](#cite_note-148)


 The berries are edible and very sweet.
 [[73]](#cite_note-fna-73)

***Prosopis***
 is a
 [genus](/w/index.php?title=Genus&action=edit&redlink=1 "Genus (does not exist)") 
 of
 [flowering plants](/w/index.php?title=Flowering_plant&action=edit&redlink=1 "Flowering plant (does not exist)") 
 in the
 [pea](/w/index.php?title=Pea&action=edit&redlink=1 "Pea (does not exist)") 
 family,
 [Fabaceae](/w/index.php?title=Fabaceae&action=edit&redlink=1 "Fabaceae (does not exist)") 
 . It contains around 45 species of
 [spiny](/w/index.php?title=Thorns,_spines,_and_prickles&action=edit&redlink=1 "Thorns, spines, and prickles (does not exist)") 
[trees](/w/index.php?title=Tree&action=edit&redlink=1 "Tree (does not exist)") 
 and
 [shrubs](/w/index.php?title=Shrub&action=edit&redlink=1 "Shrub (does not exist)") 
 found in
 [subtropical](/w/index.php?title=Subtropics&action=edit&redlink=1 "Subtropics (does not exist)") 
 and
 [tropical](/w/index.php?title=Tropics&action=edit&redlink=1 "Tropics (does not exist)") 
 regions of the Americas, Africa, Western Asia, and South Asia. They often thrive in
 [arid](/w/index.php?title=Arid&action=edit&redlink=1 "Arid (does not exist)") 
[soil](/w/index.php?title=Soil&action=edit&redlink=1 "Soil (does not exist)") 
 and are resistant to
 [drought](/w/index.php?title=Drought&action=edit&redlink=1 "Drought (does not exist)") 
 , on occasion developing extremely deep
 [root](/w/index.php?title=Root&action=edit&redlink=1 "Root (does not exist)") 
 systems. Their
 [wood](/w/index.php?title=Wood&action=edit&redlink=1 "Wood (does not exist)") 
 is usually hard, dense and durable. Their
 [fruits](/w/index.php?title=Fruit&action=edit&redlink=1 "Fruit (does not exist)") 
 are
 [pods](/w/index.php?title=Legume&action=edit&redlink=1 "Legume (does not exist)") 
 and may contain large amounts of
 [sugar](/w/index.php?title=Sugar&action=edit&redlink=1 "Sugar (does not exist)") 
 . The generic name means "
 [burdock](/w/index.php?title=Burdock&action=edit&redlink=1 "Burdock (does not exist)") 
 " in
 [late Latin](/w/index.php?title=Late_Latin&action=edit&redlink=1 "Late Latin (does not exist)") 
 and originated in the Greek language.
 [[149]](#cite_note-149)


 The species
 *[Prosopis pallida](/w/index.php?title=Prosopis_pallida&action=edit&redlink=1 "Prosopis pallida (does not exist)")*
 was introduced to
 [Hawaii](/w/index.php?title=Hawaii&action=edit&redlink=1 "Hawaii (does not exist)") 
 in 1828 and now dominates many of the drier coastal parts of the islands, where it is called the
 [kiawe tree](/w/index.php?title=Kiawe_tree&action=edit&redlink=1 "Kiawe tree (does not exist)") 
 and is a prime source of
 [monofloral honey](/w/index.php?title=Monofloral_honey&action=edit&redlink=1 "Monofloral honey (does not exist)") 
 production.
 [[150]](#cite_note-150)


 In Australia, invasive
 *Prosopis* 
 species are causing severe economic and environmental damage. With their thorns and many low branches,
 *Prosopis* 
 shrubs form impenetrable thickets which prevent cattle from accessing watering holes, etc. They also take over pastoral grasslands and suck up scarce water.
 *Prosopis* 
 species cause land erosion due to loss of grassland that are habitats for native plants and animals.
 *Prosopis* 
 thickets also provide shelter for feral animals such as pigs and cats.
 [[151]](#cite_note-environment.gov.au-151)



 |
| 





***[Quercus](/w/index.php?title=Wildlife_Gardening/Taxon/Quercus&action=edit&redlink=1 "Wildlife Gardening/Taxon/Quercus (does not exist)")***
 , or oak.
 *[Q. alba](/w/index.php?title=Wildlife_Gardening/Taxon/Quercus_alba&action=edit&redlink=1 "Wildlife Gardening/Taxon/Quercus alba (does not exist)")*
 , the white oak, pictured.

 6 !
 



Beneficial for this purpose



 |

Little to no effect

 Overwintering roost.
 [[132]](#cite_note-Robert_Michael_Pyle-132)
 Information on native range, cultivation requirements, bloom time and any other criteria that might lead a gardener to select this species over the others.
 

 The genus
 *Quercus* 
 is native to the Northern Hemisphere, and includes
 [deciduous](/w/index.php?title=Deciduous&action=edit&redlink=1 "Deciduous (does not exist)") 
 and
 [evergreen](/w/index.php?title=Evergreen&action=edit&redlink=1 "Evergreen (does not exist)") 
 species extending from
 [cool temperate](/w/index.php?title=Temperateness&action=edit&redlink=1 "Temperateness (does not exist)") 
 to
 [tropical](/w/index.php?title=Tropical&action=edit&redlink=1 "Tropical (does not exist)") 
 latitudes in the Americas, Asia, Europe, and North Africa. North America contains the largest number of oak species, with approximately 90 occurring in the United States, while Mexico has 160 species of which 109 are endemic. The second greatest center of oak diversity is China, which contains approximately 100 species.
 [[152]](#cite_note-152)


 Oaks have
 [spirally](/w/index.php?title=Spiral&action=edit&redlink=1 "Spiral (does not exist)") 
 arranged leaves, with
 [lobate margins](/w/index.php?title=Dentate_leaf&action=edit&redlink=1 "Dentate leaf (does not exist)") 
 in many species; some have
 [serrated leaves](/w/index.php?title=Serrated_leaves&action=edit&redlink=1 "Serrated leaves (does not exist)") 
 or entire leaves with smooth margins. Many deciduous species are
 [marcescent](/w/index.php?title=Marcescent&action=edit&redlink=1 "Marcescent (does not exist)") 
 , not dropping dead leaves until spring. In spring, a single oak tree produces both male flowers (in the form of
 [catkins](/w/index.php?title=Catkin&action=edit&redlink=1 "Catkin (does not exist)") 
 ) and small female flowers.
 [[153]](#cite_note-153)


 The fruit is a
 [nut](/w/index.php?title=Nut_(fruit)&action=edit&redlink=1 "Nut (fruit) (does not exist)") 
 called an
 [acorn](/w/index.php?title=Acorn&action=edit&redlink=1 "Acorn (does not exist)") 
 or oak nut borne in a cup-like structure known as a
 [cupule](/w/index.php?title=Calybium_and_cupule&action=edit&redlink=1 "Calybium and cupule (does not exist)") 
 ; each acorn contains one seed (rarely two or three) and takes 6–18 months to mature, depending on their species. The acorns and leaves contain
 [tannic acid](/w/index.php?title=Tannic_acid&action=edit&redlink=1 "Tannic acid (does not exist)") 
 ,
 [[154]](#cite_note-154)


 Oaks are
 [keystone species](/w/index.php?title=Keystone_species&action=edit&redlink=1 "Keystone species (does not exist)") 
 in a wide range of habitats from Mediterranean semi-desert to subtropical rainforest. For example, oak trees are important components of hardwood forests, and certain species are particularly known to grow in associations with members of the
 [Ericaceae](/w/index.php?title=Ericaceae&action=edit&redlink=1 "Ericaceae (does not exist)") 
 in
 [oak–heath forests](/w/index.php?title=Oak%E2%80%93heath_forest&action=edit&redlink=1 "Oak–heath forest (does not exist)") 
 .
 [[155]](#cite_note-r10-155)
[[156]](#cite_note-r11-156)


 The mature trees shed varying numbers of acorns annually. Scientists suggest that shedding excess numbers allows the oaks to satiate nut gathering species which improves the chances of germination. Every four to ten years, certain oak populations will synchronize to produce almost no acorns at all, only to rain them down excessively the following year, known as a "mast" year. The year preceding the mast year is thought to starve off the mammal populations feeding on the supply, thereby increasing the effectiveness of the overproduction in the mast year that follows.
 [[157]](#cite_note-157)
[[158]](#cite_note-158)
 This is necessary to the survival of any given oak species, as only one in 10,000 acorns results in an eventual tree.
 [[159]](#cite_note-159)
 |
| 





***[Rhus](/w/index.php?title=Wildlife_Gardening/Taxon/Rhus&action=edit&redlink=1 "Wildlife Gardening/Taxon/Rhus (does not exist)")***
 , or sumacs.

 , the staghorn sumac, pictured.

 Overwintering roost.
 [[132]](#cite_note-Robert_Michael_Pyle-132)
 Information on native range, cultivation requirements, bloom time and any other criteria that might lead a gardener to select this species over the others.
 

***Rhus typhina***
 , the
 **staghorn sumac** 
 ,
 [[160]](#cite_note-160)
 is a
 [species](/w/index.php?title=Species&action=edit&redlink=1 "Species (does not exist)") 
 of
 [flowering plant](/w/index.php?title=Flowering_plant&action=edit&redlink=1 "Flowering plant (does not exist)") 
 in the
 [family](/w/index.php?title=Family_(biology)&action=edit&redlink=1 "Family (biology) (does not exist)") 
 Anacardiaceae,
 [native](/w/index.php?title=Native_plant&action=edit&redlink=1 "Native plant (does not exist)") 
 to eastern North America. It is primarily found in southeastern Canada, the northeastern and midwestern United States and the Appalachian Mountains,
 [[161]](#cite_note-161)
 but is widely cultivated as an ornamental throughout the
 [temperate](/w/index.php?title=Temperate&action=edit&redlink=1 "Temperate (does not exist)") 
 world.
 

*Rhus typhina* 
 is a
 [dioecious](/w/index.php?title=Plant_sexuality&action=edit&redlink=1 "Plant sexuality (does not exist)") 
 ,
 [deciduous](/w/index.php?title=Deciduous&action=edit&redlink=1 "Deciduous (does not exist)") 
[shrub](/w/index.php?title=Shrub&action=edit&redlink=1 "Shrub (does not exist)") 
 or small
 [tree](/w/index.php?title=Tree&action=edit&redlink=1 "Tree (does not exist)") 
 growing up to
 [Template:Cvt](/w/index.php?title=Template:Cvt&action=edit&redlink=1 "Template:Cvt (does not exist)") 
 tall by
 [Template:Cvt](/w/index.php?title=Template:Cvt&action=edit&redlink=1 "Template:Cvt (does not exist)") 
 broad. It has alternate, pinnately compound leaves
 [Template:Cvt](/w/index.php?title=Template:Cvt&action=edit&redlink=1 "Template:Cvt (does not exist)") 
 long, each with 9–31 serrate leaflets
 [Template:Cvt](/w/index.php?title=Template:Cvt&action=edit&redlink=1 "Template:Cvt (does not exist)") 
 long.
 [[162]](#cite_note-Uva-162)



 Small, greenish-white through yellowish flowers occur in dense terminal panicles, and small, green through reddish
 [drupes](/w/index.php?title=Drupes&action=edit&redlink=1 "Drupes (does not exist)") 
 occur in dense infructescences.
 [[162]](#cite_note-Uva-162)
 Flowers occur from May through July and fruit ripens from June through September in this species’ native range.
 [[163]](#cite_note-163)
 Infructescences are
 [Template:Cvt](/w/index.php?title=Template:Cvt&action=edit&redlink=1 "Template:Cvt (does not exist)") 
 long and
 [Template:Cvt](/w/index.php?title=Template:Cvt&action=edit&redlink=1 "Template:Cvt (does not exist)") 
 broad at their bases. Fall foliage is brilliant shades of red, orange and yellow.
 [[70]](#cite_note-RHSAZ-70)
 Fruit can remain on plants from late summer through spring. It is eaten by many birds in winter.
 [[164]](#cite_note-164)


 Staghorn sumac spreads by seeds and
 [rhizomes](/w/index.php?title=Rhizomes&action=edit&redlink=1 "Rhizomes (does not exist)") 
 and forms clones often with the older shoots in the center and younger shoots around central older ones.
 [[162]](#cite_note-Uva-162)
 Large clones can grow from
 
 octets
 
[
 *[clarification needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Please_clarify "wikipedia:Please clarify")*
 ] 
 in several years.
 

 Staghorn sumac is an ornamental plant which provides interest throughout the year; though its vigorous, suckering habit makes it unsuitable for smaller gardens. It can grow under a wide array of conditions, but is most often found in dry and poor soil on which other plants cannot survive.
 [[162]](#cite_note-Uva-162)
 Some landscapers remove all but the top branches to create a "crown" effect in order to resemble a small palm tree. Numerous
 [cultivars](/w/index.php?title=Cultivars&action=edit&redlink=1 "Cultivars (does not exist)") 
 have been developed for garden use, of which 'Dissecta' [[synonym (taxonomy)|syn. 'Laciniata' (
 **cutleaf staghorn sumac** 
 ) has gained the
 [Royal Horticultural Society](/w/index.php?title=Royal_Horticultural_Society&action=edit&redlink=1 "Royal Horticultural Society (does not exist)") 
 ’s
 [Award of Garden Merit](/w/index.php?title=Award_of_Garden_Merit&action=edit&redlink=1 "Award of Garden Merit (does not exist)") 
 .
 [[165]](#cite_note-RHSPF-165)
[[166]](#cite_note-166)






 |

 , or willow.

 , the Goodding's willow, pictured.

 [[132]](#cite_note-Robert_Michael_Pyle-132)
 Information on native range, cultivation requirements, bloom time and any other criteria that might lead a gardener to select this species over the others.
 

**Willows** 
 , also called
 **sallows** 
 and
 **osiers** 
 , form the
 [genus](/w/index.php?title=Genus&action=edit&redlink=1 "Genus (does not exist)") 
***Salix***
 , around 400 species
 [[167]](#cite_note-Mabberley-167)
 of
 [deciduous](/w/index.php?title=Deciduous&action=edit&redlink=1 "Deciduous (does not exist)") 
 trees and
 [shrubs](/w/index.php?title=Shrub&action=edit&redlink=1 "Shrub (does not exist)") 
 , found primarily on moist
 [soils](/w/index.php?title=Soil&action=edit&redlink=1 "Soil (does not exist)") 
 in cold and
 [temperate](/w/index.php?title=Temperate&action=edit&redlink=1 "Temperate (does not exist)") 
 regions of the
 [Northern Hemisphere](/w/index.php?title=Northern_Hemisphere&action=edit&redlink=1 "Northern Hemisphere (does not exist)") 
 .
 

 Almost all willows take root very readily from
 [cuttings](/w/index.php?title=Cutting_(plant)&action=edit&redlink=1 "Cutting (plant) (does not exist)") 
 or where broken branches lie on the ground.
 

 Willows are used as food plants by the
 [larvae](/w/index.php?title=Larva&action=edit&redlink=1 "Larva (does not exist)") 
 of some
 [Lepidoptera](/w/index.php?title=Lepidoptera&action=edit&redlink=1 "Lepidoptera (does not exist)") 
[species](/w/index.php?title=List_of_Lepidoptera_that_feed_on_willows&action=edit&redlink=1 "List of Lepidoptera that feed on willows (does not exist)") 
 , such as the
 [mourning cloak butterfly](/w/index.php?title=Nymphalis_antiopa&action=edit&redlink=1 "Nymphalis antiopa (does not exist)") 
 .
 [[168]](#cite_note-168)
 Ants, such as
 [wood ants](/w/index.php?title=Formica_rufa_group&action=edit&redlink=1 "Formica rufa group (does not exist)") 
 , are common on willows inhabited by
 [aphids](/w/index.php?title=Aphid&action=edit&redlink=1 "Aphid (does not exist)") 
 , coming to collect aphid
 [honeydew](/w/index.php?title=Honeydew_(secretion)&action=edit&redlink=1 "Honeydew (secretion) (does not exist)") 
 , as sometimes do
 [wasps](/w/index.php?title=Wasp&action=edit&redlink=1 "Wasp (does not exist)") 
 .
 

 The
 [leaves](/w/index.php?title=Leaf&action=edit&redlink=1 "Leaf (does not exist)") 
 are typically elongated, but may also be round to oval, frequently with serrated edges. Most species are
 [deciduous](/w/index.php?title=Deciduous&action=edit&redlink=1 "Deciduous (does not exist)") 
 ; semi
 [evergreen](/w/index.php?title=Evergreen&action=edit&redlink=1 "Evergreen (does not exist)") 
 willows with
 [coriaceous](/w/index.php?title=Coriaceous&action=edit&redlink=1 "Coriaceous (does not exist)") 
 leaves are rare, e.g.
 *Salix micans* 
 and
 *S. australior* 
 in the eastern
 [Mediterranean](/w/index.php?title=Mediterranean&action=edit&redlink=1 "Mediterranean (does not exist)") 
 . All the
 [buds](/w/index.php?title=Bud&action=edit&redlink=1 "Bud (does not exist)") 
 are lateral; no absolutely terminal bud is ever formed. The buds are covered by a single scale. Usually, the bud scale is fused into a cap-like shape, but in some species it wraps around and the edges overlap.
 [[169]](#cite_note-169)


 The leaves are simple, feather-veined, and typically linear-lanceolate. Usually they are serrate, rounded at base, acute or acuminate.
 

 In color, the leaves show a great variety of greens, ranging from yellowish to bluish color. Willows are among the earliest woody plants to leaf out in spring and the last to drop their leaves in autumn. Leafout may occur as early as February depending on the climate and is stimulated by air temperature. If daytime highs reach 55 °F (10 °C) for a few consecutive days, a willow will attempt to put out leaves and flowers. Leaf drop in autumn occurs when day length shortens to approximately ten hours and 25 minutes, which varies by latitude (as early as the first week of October for boreal species such as
 *S. alaxensis* 
 and as late as the third week of December for willows growing in far southern areas).
 

 Willows are
 [dioecious](/w/index.php?title=Dioecious&action=edit&redlink=1 "Dioecious (does not exist)") 
 , with male and female
 [flowers](/w/index.php?title=Flower&action=edit&redlink=1 "Flower (does not exist)") 
 appearing as
 [catkins](/w/index.php?title=Catkin&action=edit&redlink=1 "Catkin (does not exist)") 
 on separate plants; the catkins are produced early in the spring, often before the leaves.
  |
| 





***[Syringa vulgaris](/w/index.php?title=Wildlife_Gardening/Taxon/Syringa_vulgaris&action=edit&redlink=1 "Wildlife Gardening/Taxon/Syringa vulgaris (does not exist)")***
 , or lilac.

 Nectar source.
 [[11]](#cite_note-isc-11)
 Information on native range, cultivation requirements, bloom time and any other criteria that might lead a gardener to select this species over the others.
 

***Syringa vulgaris***
 (
 **lilac** 
 or
 **common lilac** 
 ) is a
 [species](/w/index.php?title=Species&action=edit&redlink=1 "Species (does not exist)") 
 of
 [flowering plant](/w/index.php?title=Flowering_plant&action=edit&redlink=1 "Flowering plant (does not exist)") 
 in the olive
 [family](/w/index.php?title=Family_(biology)&action=edit&redlink=1 "Family (biology) (does not exist)") 
 Oleaceae,
 [native](/w/index.php?title=Native_plant&action=edit&redlink=1 "Native plant (does not exist)") 
 to the
 [Balkan Peninsula](/w/index.php?title=Balkan_Peninsula&action=edit&redlink=1 "Balkan Peninsula (does not exist)") 
 , where it grows on rocky hills.
 [[170]](#cite_note-rushforth-170)
[[171]](#cite_note-mc-171)
[[172]](#cite_note-fe-172)
 Grown for its scented pink flowers in spring, this large shrub or small tree is widely cultivated and has been naturalized in parts of Europe and North America. It is not regarded as an aggressive species, found in the wild in widely scattered sites, usually in the vicinity of past or present human habitations.
 [[173]](#cite_note-173)
[[174]](#cite_note-174)
[[175]](#cite_note-175)


*Syringa vulgaris* 
 is a large
 [deciduous](/w/index.php?title=Deciduous&action=edit&redlink=1 "Deciduous (does not exist)") 
[shrub](/w/index.php?title=Shrub&action=edit&redlink=1 "Shrub (does not exist)") 
 or multistemmed small
 [tree](/w/index.php?title=Tree&action=edit&redlink=1 "Tree (does not exist)") 
 , growing to 6–7 m (20–23 ft) high.
 

 The lilac is a very popular
 [ornamental plant](/w/index.php?title=Ornamental_plant&action=edit&redlink=1 "Ornamental plant (does not exist)") 
 in gardens and parks, because of its attractive, sweet-smelling flowers, which appear in early summer just before many of the roses and other summer flowers come into bloom.
 [[70]](#cite_note-RHSAZ-70)


 In late summer, lilacs can be attacked by
 [powdery mildew](/w/index.php?title=Powdery_mildew&action=edit&redlink=1 "Powdery mildew (does not exist)") 
 , specifically
 *Erysiphe syringae* 
 , one of the
 [Erysiphaceae](/w/index.php?title=Erysiphaceae&action=edit&redlink=1 "Erysiphaceae (does not exist)") 
 .
 [[176]](#cite_note-176)
 No fall color is seen and the seed clusters have no aesthetic appeal.
 

 Common lilac tends to flower profusely in alternate years, a habit that can be improved by
 [deadheading](/w/index.php?title=Deadheading_(flowers)&action=edit&redlink=1 "Deadheading (flowers) (does not exist)") 
 the flower clusters after the color has faded and before seeds, few of which are fertile, form. At the same time, twiggy growth on shoots that have flowered more than once or twice can be cut to a strong, outward-growing side shoot.
 

 It is widely
 [naturalised](/w/index.php?title=Naturalisation_(biology)&action=edit&redlink=1 "Naturalisation (biology) (does not exist)") 
 in western and northern Europe.
 [[177]](#cite_note-blamey-177)



 |
| 





***[Tilia americana](/w/index.php?title=Wildlife_Gardening/Taxon/Tilia_americana&action=edit&redlink=1 "Wildlife Gardening/Taxon/Tilia americana (does not exist)")***
 , or basswood.
  |

 |

 Overwintering roost.
 [[132]](#cite_note-Robert_Michael_Pyle-132)
 Information on native range, cultivation requirements, bloom time and any other criteria that might lead a gardener to select this species over the others.
 

***Tilia americana***
 is a
 [species](/w/index.php?title=Species&action=edit&redlink=1 "Species (does not exist)") 
 of
 [tree](/w/index.php?title=Tree&action=edit&redlink=1 "Tree (does not exist)") 
 in the
 [family](/w/index.php?title=Family_(biology)&action=edit&redlink=1 "Family (biology) (does not exist)") 
[Malvaceae](/w/index.php?title=Malvaceae&action=edit&redlink=1 "Malvaceae (does not exist)") 
 , native to eastern
 [North America](/w/index.php?title=North_America&action=edit&redlink=1 "North America (does not exist)") 
 , from southeast
 [Manitoba](/w/index.php?title=Manitoba&action=edit&redlink=1 "Manitoba (does not exist)") 
 east to
 [New Brunswick](/w/index.php?title=New_Brunswick&action=edit&redlink=1 "New Brunswick (does not exist)") 
 , southwest to northeast
 [Oklahoma](/w/index.php?title=Oklahoma&action=edit&redlink=1 "Oklahoma (does not exist)") 
 , southeast to
 [South Carolina](/w/index.php?title=South_Carolina&action=edit&redlink=1 "South Carolina (does not exist)") 
 , and west along the
 [Niobrara River](/w/index.php?title=Niobrara_River&action=edit&redlink=1 "Niobrara River (does not exist)") 
 to
 [Cherry County, Nebraska](/w/index.php?title=Cherry_County,_Nebraska&action=edit&redlink=1 "Cherry County, Nebraska (does not exist)") 
 . It is the sole representative of its genus in the Western Hemisphere, assuming
 *T. caroliniana* 
 is treated as a subspecies or local ecotype of
 *T. americana* 
 .
 [[178]](#cite_note-usfs-178)
[[52]](#cite_note-grin-52)


 The American basswood is a medium-sized to large
 [deciduous](/w/index.php?title=Deciduous&action=edit&redlink=1 "Deciduous (does not exist)") 
[tree](/w/index.php?title=Tree&action=edit&redlink=1 "Tree (does not exist)") 
 reaching a height of 18 to 37 m (59 to 121 ft) exceptionally 39 m (128 ft) with a trunk diameter of 1–1.5 m (3–5 ft) at maturity. It grows faster than many North American hardwoods, often twice the annual growth rate of
 [American beech](/w/index.php?title=American_beech&action=edit&redlink=1 "American beech (does not exist)") 
 and many
 [birch](/w/index.php?title=Birch&action=edit&redlink=1 "Birch (does not exist)") 
 species. Life expectancy is around 200 years, with flowering and seeding generally occurring between 15 and 100 years, though occasionally seed production may start as early as 8 years.
 

 The
 [flowers](/w/index.php?title=Flower&action=edit&redlink=1 "Flower (does not exist)") 
 are small, fragrant, yellowish-white, 10–14 mm (0.39–0.55 inch) in diameter, arranged in drooping,
 [cymose](/w/index.php?title=Cymose&action=edit&redlink=1 "Cymose (does not exist)") 
 clusters of 6–20 with a whitish-green leaf-like
 [bract](/w/index.php?title=Bract&action=edit&redlink=1 "Bract (does not exist)") 
 attached for half its length at the base of the cyme.
 

 Time of flowering varies by several weeks depending on the latitude; early May in Canada and early June in the extreme southern extent. Leaf drop in fall occurs between early and late October depending on the latitude. The flowers are fragrant and insect-pollinated.
 

 American basswood is dominant in the sugar maple–basswood forest association, which is most common in western
 [Wisconsin](/w/index.php?title=Wisconsin&action=edit&redlink=1 "Wisconsin (does not exist)") 
 and central
 [Minnesota](/w/index.php?title=Minnesota&action=edit&redlink=1 "Minnesota (does not exist)") 
 , but occurs as far east as
 [New England](/w/index.php?title=New_England&action=edit&redlink=1 "New England (does not exist)") 
 and southern
 [Quebec](/w/index.php?title=Quebec&action=edit&redlink=1 "Quebec (does not exist)") 
 in places that have
 [mesic](/w/index.php?title=Mesic_habitat&action=edit&redlink=1 "Mesic habitat (does not exist)") 
 soil with relatively high
 [pH](/w/index.php?title=Soil_pH&action=edit&redlink=1 "Soil pH (does not exist)") 
 . It also has minor occurrence in many other forest cover types.
 

 Its flowers provide abundant nectar for insects. The seeds are eaten by
 [chipmunks](/w/index.php?title=Chipmunk&action=edit&redlink=1 "Chipmunk (does not exist)") 
 ,
 [mice](/w/index.php?title=Mice&action=edit&redlink=1 "Mice (does not exist)") 
 , and
 [squirrels](/w/index.php?title=Squirrel&action=edit&redlink=1 "Squirrel (does not exist)") 
 .
 [Rabbits](/w/index.php?title=Rabbit&action=edit&redlink=1 "Rabbit (does not exist)") 
 and
 [voles](/w/index.php?title=Vole&action=edit&redlink=1 "Vole (does not exist)") 
 eat the bark, sometimes girdling young trees. The leaves serve as food for
 [caterpillars](/w/index.php?title=Caterpillar&action=edit&redlink=1 "Caterpillar (does not exist)") 
 of various
 [Lepidoptera](/w/index.php?title=Lepidoptera&action=edit&redlink=1 "Lepidoptera (does not exist)") 
 (see
 [Lepidoptera which feed on
 *Tilia*](/w/index.php?title=List_of_Lepidoptera_that_feed_on_Tilia&action=edit&redlink=1 "List of Lepidoptera that feed on Tilia (does not exist)")
 ). The
 [ribbed cocoon maker](/w/index.php?title=Bucculatricidae&action=edit&redlink=1 "Bucculatricidae (does not exist)") 
 species
 *[Bucculatrix improvisa](/w/index.php?title=Bucculatrix_improvisa&action=edit&redlink=1 "Bucculatrix improvisa (does not exist)")*
 has not been found on other plants.
 

 This species is particularly susceptible to adult
 [Japanese beetles](/w/index.php?title=Japanese_beetle&action=edit&redlink=1 "Japanese beetle (does not exist)") 
 (an
 [invasive species](/w/index.php?title=Invasive_species&action=edit&redlink=1 "Invasive species (does not exist)") 
 in North America) that feed on its leaves.
 [[179]](#cite_note-179)


 The American basswood is known for being one of the most difficult native North American trees to propagate from seed, as they not only have a low viability rate (approximately 30% of all seeds are viable), but quickly develop an extremely hard seed coating that may delay germination for up to two years. If planting them, it is recommended to gather the seeds in early autumn and sow them before they dry out and form a coating. This will then allow germination to occur immediately. Overall, seeds are not a major part of the tree's reproductive strategy and it instead mostly spreads by self-coppicing. All juvenile basswoods
 [coppice](/w/index.php?title=Coppice&action=edit&redlink=1 "Coppice (does not exist)") 
 extremely readily, and even old trees will often sprout from the stump if cut.
 

 The American basswood is recommended as an ornamental tree when the mass of foliage or a deep shade is desired; no native tree surpasses it in this respect. It is often planted on the windward side of an orchard as a protection to young and delicate trees.
 [[141]](#cite_note-Keeler-141)
 It is cultivated at least as far north as
 [Juneau, Alaska](/w/index.php?title=Juneau,_Alaska&action=edit&redlink=1 "Juneau, Alaska (does not exist)") 
 .
 [[180]](#cite_note-180)


 The foliage and flowers are both edible, though the tender young leaves are more palatable.
  |
| 





***[Ulmus](/w/index.php?title=Wildlife_Gardening/Taxon/Ulmus&action=edit&redlink=1 "Wildlife Gardening/Taxon/Ulmus (does not exist)")***
 , or elms.
 *[U. americana](/w/index.php?title=Wildlife_Gardening/Taxon/Ulmus_americana&action=edit&redlink=1 "Wildlife Gardening/Taxon/Ulmus americana (does not exist)")*
 , the American elm, pictured.

 [[132]](#cite_note-Robert_Michael_Pyle-132)
 Information on native range, cultivation requirements, bloom time and any other criteria that might lead a gardener to select this species over the others.
 

**Elms** 
 are
 [deciduous](/w/index.php?title=Deciduous&action=edit&redlink=1 "Deciduous (does not exist)") 
 and
 [semi-deciduous](/w/index.php?title=Semi-deciduous&action=edit&redlink=1 "Semi-deciduous (does not exist)") 
[trees](/w/index.php?title=Tree&action=edit&redlink=1 "Tree (does not exist)") 
 comprising the
 [flowering plant](/w/index.php?title=Flowering_plant&action=edit&redlink=1 "Flowering plant (does not exist)") 
 genus
 *Ulmus* 
 in the plant family
 [Ulmaceae](/w/index.php?title=Ulmaceae&action=edit&redlink=1 "Ulmaceae (does not exist)") 
 .
 

 The genus is
 [hermaphroditic](/w/index.php?title=Hermaphroditic&action=edit&redlink=1 "Hermaphroditic (does not exist)") 
 , having
 [apetalous](/w/index.php?title=Apetalous&action=edit&redlink=1 "Apetalous (does not exist)") 
[perfect flowers](/w/index.php?title=Perfect_flower&action=edit&redlink=1 "Perfect flower (does not exist)") 
 which are wind-pollinated. Elm
 [leaves](/w/index.php?title=Leaves&action=edit&redlink=1 "Leaves (does not exist)") 
 are alternate, with simple, single- or, most commonly,
 [doubly serrate](/w/index.php?title=Leaf_shape&action=edit&redlink=1 "Leaf shape (does not exist)") 
 margins, usually asymmetric at the base and
 [acuminate](/w/index.php?title=Acuminate&action=edit&redlink=1 "Acuminate (does not exist)") 
 at the
 [apex](https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/apex "wikt:apex") 
 . The fruit is a round wind-dispersed
 [samara](/w/index.php?title=Samara_(fruit)&action=edit&redlink=1 "Samara (fruit) (does not exist)") 
 flushed with
 [chlorophyll](/w/index.php?title=Chlorophyll&action=edit&redlink=1 "Chlorophyll (does not exist)") 
 , facilitating photosynthesis before the leaves emerge.
 [[181]](#cite_note-Heybroek-181)
 The samarae are very light, those of British elms numbering around 50,000 to the
 [pound](/w/index.php?title=Pound_(weight)&action=edit&redlink=1 "Pound (weight) (does not exist)") 
 (454
 [g](/w/index.php?title=Gram&action=edit&redlink=1 "Gram (does not exist)") 
 ).
 [[182]](#cite_note-Edlin-182)
 All species are tolerant of a wide range of soils and
 [pH](/w/index.php?title=PH&action=edit&redlink=1 "PH (does not exist)") 
 levels but, with few exceptions, demand good drainage. The elm tree can grow to great height, often with a forked trunk creating a vase profile.

**[Hymenoptera](/w/index.php?title=Wildlife_Gardening/Taxon/Hymenoptera&action=edit&redlink=1 "Wildlife Gardening/Taxon/Hymenoptera (does not exist)")**
 , the order including wasps.

 , or the eastern yellowjacket pictured.
  | 

 4 !

 4 !

Little to no effect



 | 

 2 !
 



Detrimental for this purpose



 | 

 4 !

 | 
 A variety of wasps will prey upon caterpillars.

 Information on native range or any other factor that might influence monarchs' likelihood of attracting this species to a garden.

***[Tenodera sinensis](/w/index.php?title=Wildlife_Gardening/Taxon/Tenodera_sinensis&action=edit&redlink=1 "Wildlife Gardening/Taxon/Tenodera sinensis (does not exist)")***
 , the Chinese mantis. Male pictured.

Little to no effect

Little to no effect



 | 

 2 !
 



Detrimental for this purpose



 | 

 4 !
 



Little to no effect



 | 
 Preys upon caterpillars and avoids being poisoned by not eating its prey's digestive tract and the processed milkweed material it contains.
 [[186]](#cite_note-186)
 Information on native range or any other factor that might influence monarchs' likelihood of attracting this species to a garden.

***[Asclepias curassavica](/w/index.php?title=Wildlife_Gardening/Taxon/Asclepias_curassavica&action=edit&redlink=1 "Wildlife Gardening/Taxon/Asclepias curassavica (does not exist)")***
 , the scarlet milkweed. Flowers and leaves pictured.
  | 

 1 !
 



Very detrimental for this purpose



 | 

 6 !
 



Beneficial for this purpose



 | 

 6 !
 



Beneficial for this purpose



 | 

 4 !
 



Little to no effect



 | 

 4 !
 



Little to no effect



 | 
 Year-round plantings in the USA are controversial and criticised, as they may be the cause of new overwintering sites along the U.S. Gulf Coast, leading to year-round breeding of monarchs.
 [[191]](#cite_note-191)
 This is thought to adversely affect migration patterns, and to cause a dramatic buildup of the dangerous parasite,
 *[Ophryocystis elektroscirrha](/w/index.php?title=Ophryocystis_elektroscirrha&action=edit&redlink=1 "Ophryocystis elektroscirrha (does not exist)")*
 .
 [[192]](#cite_note-192)
 . New research also has shown that monarch larvae reared on tropical milkweed show reduced migratory development (reproductive diapause), and when migratory adults are exposed to tropical milkweed, it stimulates reproductive tissue growth -
 <https://www.mdpi.com/2075-4450/10/8/253/htm>
 Information on native range, cultivation requirements, bloom time and any other criteria that might lead a gardener to select this species over the others.
  |

***[Vincetoxicum nigrum](/w/index.php?title=Wildlife_Gardening/Taxon/Vincetoxicum_nigrum&action=edit&redlink=1 "Wildlife Gardening/Taxon/Vincetoxicum nigrum (does not exist)")***

 1 !

 1 !
 



Very detrimental for this purpose



 | 

 1 !
 



Very detrimental for this purpose

Little to no effect



 | 
 This plant is sufficiently closely related to vining milkweeeds to trick female monarchs into laying eggs on it, although it is toxic to monarch caterpillars. It is regarded as an invasive species in North America.
 [[193]](#cite_note-193)
 Information on native range, cultivation requirements, bloom time and any other criteria that might lead a gardener to select this species over the others.

***[Crataegus crus-galli](/w/index.php?title=Wildlife_Gardening/Taxon/Crataegus_crus-galli&action=edit&redlink=1 "Wildlife Gardening/Taxon/Crataegus crus-galli (does not exist)")***

 Especially attractive to bees, including multiple
 *[Andrena](/w/index.php?title=Wildlife_Gardening/Taxon/Andrena&action=edit&redlink=1 "Wildlife Gardening/Taxon/Andrena (does not exist)")*
 species ("mining bees"), bumble bees, and honey bees. Birds are also attracted to this tree.
 [[1]](#cite_note-midatlantic-195)
 May be moderately allelopathic.
 [[2]](#cite_note-coder-99-196)
 |
|

***[Juniperus virginiana](/w/index.php?title=Wildlife_Gardening/Taxon/Juniperus_virginiana&action=edit&redlink=1 "Wildlife Gardening/Taxon/Juniperus virginiana (does not exist)")***

 cover for wildlife. bird nesting site. bird food. winter bird food. winter food for cedar waxwings. food for breeding birds. caterpillar host plant. many specialists like juniper hairstreak. evergreen screen. shade tree. "berry-like cones used for flavoring gin".

 wildlife cover. bird nest. winter bird food. breeding bird food. spring bird food. mammal food. caterpillar host plant. evergreen screen. shade tree. many mammals many birds more than 210 species of caterpillar.

Beneficial for this purpose



 | 

 4 !
 



Little to no effect



 | 
 wildlife cover. pollen. nectar. winter bird food. mammal food. spring flowers. summer flowers. evergreen groundcover. many birds and mammals eat berries.
  |
|
